<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="learning-objectives">LEARNING OBJECTIVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>After reading this chapter, you should be able to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain holistic gerontological nursing care.</li>
<li>Describe the needs of older adults pertaining to the promotion of health and the management of health challenges.</li>
<li>List the requisites that influence older persons’ abilities to meet self-care needs.</li>
<li>Describe the general types of nursing interventions that are employed when older adults present self-care deficits.</li>
<li>Describe four characteristics of nurses who function as healers.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="terms-to-know">TERMS TO KNOW<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terms-to-know" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Holistic pertains to whole person; body, mind, and spirit
Presence being totally “with” or engaged with another individual</p>
<p>Surviving to old age is a tremendous accomplishment. Basic life requirements such as obtaining adequate nutrition, keeping oneself relatively safe, and maintaining the body's normal functions have been met with some success to survive to this season of life. Older adults have confronted and overcome to varying degrees the hurdles of coping with crises, adjusting to change, and learning new skills. Throughout their lives, older individuals have faced many important decisions, such as should they:</p>
<ul>
<li>Leave their country of birth to make a fresh start in America?</li>
<li>Stay in the family business or seek a job in a local industry?</li>
<li>Risk their lives to defend a cause in which they believe?</li>
<li>Encourage their children to fight in an unpopular war?</li>
<li>Invest their entire savings in launching a business of their own?</li>
<li>Allow their children to continue their education when the children's employment would ease a serious financial hardship?</li>
</ul>
<p>Too often, nurses seek external resources to meet the needs of older persons rather than recognizing that older adults have considerable inner resources for self-care and empowering them to use these strengths. Older adults then become passive recipients of care rather than active participants. This seems unreasonable because most older adults have had a lifetime of taking care of themselves and others, making their own decisions, and meeting life's most trying challenges. They may become angry or depressed at being forced to forfeit their decision-making functions to others. They may unnecessarily develop feelings of dependency, uselessness, and powerlessness. Gerontological nurses must recognize and mobilize the strengths and capabilities of older people so that they can be responsible and active participants in, rather than objects of, care. Tapping the resources of older individuals to enable them to actively participate in their own care promotes normalcy, independence, and individuality; aids in reducing risks of secondary problems related to the reactions of older adults to an unnecessarily imposed dependent role; and honors their wisdom, experience, and capabilities.</p>
<p>KEY CONCEPT</p>
<p>Older individuals have had to be strong and resourceful to navigate the stormy waters of life. Nurses should not overlook these strengths when planning care for older adults.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="holistic-gerontological-care">HOLISTIC GERONTOLOGICAL CARE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#holistic-gerontological-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>As the word implies, holism refers to the whole person—that is, the physical, mental, emotional, social, and spiritual facets that create a being that is greater than the sum of his or her individual parts. To view a person from a holistic perspective is to recognize the uniqueness and complexities of that individual. A <strong>holistic</strong> approach to gerontological care requires that knowledge and skills from a variety of disciplines be utilized to address the physical, mental, social, and spiritual health of individuals. Holistic gerontological care is concerned with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Facilitating growth toward wholeness</li>
<li>Promoting recovery and learning from an illness</li>
<li>Maximizing quality of life when one possesses an incurable illness or disability</li>
<li>Providing peace, comfort, and dignity as death is approached</li>
</ul>
<p>In holistic care, the goal is not to treat diseases but to serve the needs of the total person through the healing of the body, mind, and spirit.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-f8df21e9.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="key-concept">KEY CONCEPT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Gerontological nurses help older individuals achieve a sense of wholeness by guiding them in understanding and finding meaning and purpose in life; facilitating harmony of the mind, body, and spirit; mobilizing their internal and external resources; and promoting self-care behaviors.</p>
<p>Health promotion and healing through a balance of the body, mind, and spirit of individuals are at the core of holistic care and have particular relevance for gerontological care. The impact of age-related changes and the effects of highly prevalent chronic conditions can easily threaten the well-being of the body, mind, and spirit; therefore, nursing interventions to reduce such threats are essential. Because chronic diseases and the effects of advanced age cannot be eliminated, healing rather than curative efforts will be most beneficial in gerontological nursing practice. Equally significant is assisting older adults toward self-discovery in their final phase of life so that they find meaning, connectedness with others, and an understanding of their place in the universe.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="holistic-assessment-of-needs">HOLISTIC ASSESSMENT OF NEEDS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#holistic-assessment-of-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are many evidence-based assessment tools that can be useful to gerontological nurses. One of the most comprehensive listings of these tools can be found at the Hartford Institute for Geriatric Nursing (see Resource listing), which includes resources for assessment of activities of daily living (ADL), hearing, sleep, sexuality, elder mistreatment, dementia, hospital admission risk, and other topics. These tools can be used to supplement the holistic assessment, which has a slightly different emphasis. Holistic assessment identifies patient needs related to health promotion and health challenges and also identifies the older adult's requisites to meet these needs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="health-promotion-related-needs">Health Promotion-Related Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-promotion-related-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The concept of health seems simple, yet it is quite complex. Viewing health as the absence of disease offers little more clarity than defining cold as the absence of hot and creates an image that begs for a more positive, broad understanding. In regard to older adults, most of whom are living with chronic conditions, this definition would relegate most of them to the ranks of the unhealthy.</p>
<p>When asked to describe the factors that contribute to health, most people would be likely to list the basic life-sustaining needs such as</p>
<p>breathing, eating, eliminating, resting, being active, and protecting oneself from risks. These are essential to maintaining the physiological balance that sustains life. However, the reality that we can have all of our physiological needs satisfied, yet still not feel well, demonstrates that physiological balance is but one component of overall health. Connection with ourselves, others, a higher power, and nature are important factors influencing health. The fulfillment of physiological needs and a sense of being connected promote well-being of the body, mind, and spirit that enables us to experience gratification through achieving purpose, pleasure, and dignity. This holistic model demonstrates that optimal health includes those activities that not only enable us to exist but also help us to realize effective, enriched lives (Fig. 7-1).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-b5d38037.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg">
FIGURE 7-1 Health promotion-related needs.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-14a6f033.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="point-to-ponder">POINT TO PONDER<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#point-to-ponder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>What does it mean to you to be healthy and whole?</p>
<p>An improved definition of health includes consideration of the root meaning of the word health: whole. Using this foundation, health is understood as a state of wholeness...an integration of body, mind, and spirit to achieve the highest possible quality of life each day (Fig. 7-2). For some individuals, this can mean exercising at the gym, engaging in challenging work, and having a personal relationship with God; for others, it can represent propelling oneself in a wheelchair to a porch, enjoying the beauty of nature, and connecting with a universal energy.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-7f4808df.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg">
FIGURE 7-2 Rather than being limited to meaning the absence of disease, health implies a wholeness and harmony of body, mind, and spirit.</p>
<p>Views of health differ not only from individual to individual but also within the same individual from one time to another. Health priorities and expectations in a 70-year-old person may not resemble what they were when that individual was half that age. Cultural and religious influences can also affect one's view of health.</p>
<p>Optimal health of older adults rests on the degree to which the needs for physiological balance, connection, and gratification are satisfied. There is the risk that in busy clinical settings, the less tangible needs of gratification and connection can be overlooked; as advocates for older adults, gerontological nurses must assure that comprehensive care is provided by not omitting these important needs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="health-challenges-related-needs">Health Challenges-Related Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-challenges-related-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An unfortunate reality is that most older adults live with at least one chronic condition that challenges their health status. In fact, most involvement that nurses have with older adults typically involves assisting them with the demands imposed by health challenges. Older adults with acute or chronic conditions have the same basic health promotion needs as healthy individuals (i.e., physiological balance, connection, and gratification); however, their conditions may create new needs such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Education: As individuals face a new diagnosis, they need to understand the condition and its care.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Counseling: A health condition can trigger a variety of feelings and impose lifestyle adjustments.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Coaching: Just as athletes and musicians require the skills of a professional who can bring out the best in them, patients, too, can benefit from efforts to improve compliance and motivation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitoring: The complexities of health care and the changing status of aging people warrant oversight from the nurse who can track progress and needs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Coordination: Older adults with a health condition often visit several health care providers; assistance with scheduling appointments, following multiple instructions, keeping all members of the team informed, and preventing conflicting treatments are often needed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Therapies: Often, health conditions are accompanied by the need for medications, exercises, special diets, and procedures. These therapies can include conventional ones that are commonly used in mainstream practice or complementary ones, such as biofeedback, herbal remedies, acupressure, and yoga. Patients may need partial or total assistance as they implement these treatments.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advocacy: There are times when older adults may need support or interception with an issue. This could involve a nurse encouraging an older adult to express her objection to a treatment that the physician and her family are pressuring her to accept or assisting a nursing home resident in contacting the state ombudsman if the resident believes there is mismanagement of his funds.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="requisites-to-meet-needs">Requisites to Meet Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#requisites-to-meet-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As straightforward and clear as the health promotion and health challenges-related needs may seem, these needs are met with varying degrees of success because they are dependent on several factors unique to the patient. Nurses assess older adults' requisites to meet needs to determine areas for intervention.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physical-mental-and-socioeconomic-abilities">Physical, Mental, and Socioeconomic Abilities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-mental-and-socioeconomic-abilities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An individual relies on several factors to meet even the most basic life demands. For example, to normally fulfill nutritional needs, a person must have the ability to experience hunger sensations; proper cognition to adequately select, prepare, and consume food; good dental status to chew food; a functional digestive tract to utilize ingested food; energy to shop and prepare food; and the funds to purchase food. Deficits in any of these areas can create risks to nutritional status. A variety of nursing interventions can be used to reduce or eliminate physical, mental, and socioeconomic deficits.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="knowledge-experience-and-skills">Knowledge, Experience, and Skills<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#knowledge-experience-and-skills" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Limitations exist when the knowledge, experience, or skills required for a given self-care action are inadequate or nonexistent. An individual with a wealth of social skills is capable of a normal, active life that includes friendship and other social interaction. People who have knowledge of the</p>
<p>hazards of cigarette smoking will be more capable of protecting themselves from health risks associated with this habit. An older man who is widowed, however, may not be able to cook and provide an adequate diet for himself, having always depended on his wife for meal preparation. Similarly, the person who has diabetes and cannot self-inject the necessary insulin may not be able to meet the therapeutic demand for insulin administration. Specific nursing considerations for enhancing self-care capacities are offered in other chapters.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="desire-and-decision-to-take-action">Desire and Decision to Take Action<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#desire-and-decision-to-take-action" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The value a person sees in performing the action, as well as the person's knowledge, attitudes, beliefs, and degree of motivation, influences the desire and decision for action. Limitations result if a person lacks desire or decides against action. If an individual is not interested in preparing and eating meals because of social isolation and loneliness, a dietary deficiency may develop. A hypertensive individual's lack of desire and decision not to forfeit potato chips and pork products in the diet because of an attitude that it is not worth the trade-off may create a real health threat. The person who is not informed of the importance of physical activity may not realize the need to arise from bed during an illness and consequently may develop complications. Values, attitudes, and beliefs are deeply established and not easily altered. Although the nurse should respect the right of individuals to make decisions affecting their lives, if limitations restrict their ability to meet self-care demands, the nurse can help by explaining the benefit of a particular action, providing information, and motivating. In some circumstances, as with an emotionally ill or mentally incompetent person, personal desires and decisions may have to be superseded by professional judgments.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>KEY CONCEPT</strong>
&gt;
&gt; There can be vastly different reasons for older adults to have a deficit in meeting a similar need. This challenges the gerontological nurse to explore the unique and sometimes subtle dynamics of each older person's life.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="gerontological-processes">GERONTOLOGICAL PROCESSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gerontological-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing">NURSING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The assessment process considers patients' effectiveness in meeting needs related to health promotion and health challenges. If the individual is successful in fulfilling needs, there is no need for nursing intervention except to reinforce the capability for self-care. When the older adult does not have the requisites to meet needs independently, however, nursing interventions are needed. Nursing interventions are directed toward empowering the older individual by strengthening self-care capacities, eliminating or minimizing self-care limitations, and providing direct services by acting for, doing for, or assisting the individual when requirements cannot be independently fulfilled (Fig. 7-3). Assessment factors pertaining to specific systems and areas of function are found in the related chapters throughout this book.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-c8ee6f7b.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg">
FIGURE 7-3 If the nurse identifies self-care deficits in the older adult for meeting health promotion- and health challenges-related needs, nursing interventions are needed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examples-of-application">EXAMPLES OF APPLICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examples-of-application" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nursing care for older persons is often associated with implementing actions when health conditions exist. When individuals face health</p>
<p>challenges, new needs frequently arise, such as administering medications, observing for specific symptoms, and performing special treatments; these needs exceed and may affect the needs related to health promotion. In geriatric nursing, consideration must be given to assessing the impact of the health challenge on the individual's self-care capacity and identifying appropriate nursing interventions to ensure that the needs related to both health promotion and the management of health challenges are adequately met. During the assessment, the nurse identifies the specific health challenges-related needs that are present and the requisites (e.g., physical capability, knowledge, and desire) that need to be addressed to strengthen self-care capacity.</p>
<p>It is significant that interventions include those actions that can empower the older individual to achieve maximum self-care in regard to health challenges-related needs. Figure 7-3 demonstrates how the holistic self-care model becomes operational in geriatric nursing practice. The cases that follow demonstrate the application of this model.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-d988b86e.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="key-concept">KEY CONCEPT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>More effort may be needed to instruct and coach an older person to perform a self-care task independently, and more time may be taken for the person to perform the task independently than would be necessary if a caregiver did the task; however, the benefits of independence to the older person's body, mind, and spirit are worth the investment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="consider-this-case">CONSIDER THIS CASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#consider-this-case" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-f0cd0fde.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<p>Mr. R, who has lived with diabetes for a long time, administers insulin daily and follows a diabetic diet. Because of a recent urologic problem, he may now need to take antibiotics daily and perform intermittent self-catheterization. During the assessment, the nurse identifies the presence of illness-imposed needs. For instance, Mr. R performs self-catheterization according to procedure and is administering his antibiotics as prescribed, but he is not adhering to his diabetic diet and alters his insulin dosage based on "how he feels that day." Mr. R has knowledge of the diabetic diet and wants to comply; however, he had depended on his wife to prepare meals, and now that she is deceased, he has difficulty cooking nutritious meals independently. He denies ever being informed of the need for regular doses of his insulin and states that he has relied on the advice of his brother-in-law, also a diabetic, who told him to "take an extra shot of insulin when he eats a lot of sweets."</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="think-critically">THINK CRITICALLY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#think-critically" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>What is the nurse's next step once Mr. R's needs have been identified?</li>
<li>What factors must be considered in exploring Mr. R's deficits in meeting his health challenges-related needs?</li>
<li>What specific actions could be planned to address Mr. R's needs?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="applying-the-holistic-model-the-case-of-mrs-d">Applying the Holistic Model: The Case of Mrs. D<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#applying-the-holistic-model-the-case-of-mrs-d" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The following case demonstrates how this model can work.</p>
<p>Mrs. D, 78 years old, was admitted to a hospital service for acute conditions with the identified problems of a fractured neck of the femur, malnutrition, and a need for a different living arrangement. Initial observation revealed a small-framed, frail-looking lady, with obvious signs of malnutrition and dehydration. She was well oriented to person, place, and time and was able to converse and answer questions coherently. Although her memory for recent events was poor, she seldom forgot to inform anyone who was interested that she neither liked nor wanted to be in the hospital. Her previous and only other hospitalization was 55 years earlier.</p>
<p>Mrs. D had been living with her husband and an unmarried sister for more than 50 years when her husband died. For the 5 years following his death, she depended heavily on her sister for emotional support and guidance. Then her sister died, which promoted feelings of anxiety, insecurity, loneliness, and depression.</p>
<p>For the year since her sister's death, she has lived alone, caring for her six-room home in the country with no assistance other than that from a neighbor who did the shopping for Mrs. D and occasionally provided her with transportation.</p>
<p>On the day of her admission to the hospital, Mrs. D had fallen on her kitchen floor, weak from her malnourished state. Discovering her hours later, her neighbor called an ambulance, which transported Mrs. D to the hospital. Once the diagnosis of fractured femur was established, plans were made to perform a nailing procedure, to correct her malnourished state, and to find a new living arrangement because her home demanded more energy and attention than she was capable of providing.</p>
<p>NURSING CARE PLAN 7-1 illustrates how Mrs. D's holistic needs directed nursing problems and related nursing actions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing-care-plan-7-1">NURSING CARE PLAN 7-1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care-plan-7-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="holistic-care-for-mrs-d">HOLISTIC CARE FOR MRS. D<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#holistic-care-for-mrs-d" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>NEEDS: Respiration and Circulation</p>
<p>Nursing Problems:(1) Mobility difficulties related to fracture and (2) impaired respiration</p>
<p>Goals: The patient demonstrates signs of adequate respiration, is free from respiratory distress and infection, and is free from signs of impaired circulation.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Maintain normal respirations.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Prevent blockage of airway or any other interference with normal breathing.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>■ Observe for and detect respiratory problems early.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Promote active and passive exercises.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Teach and encourage turning, coughing, and deep-breathing exercises.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Encourage active exercises, such as using incentive spirometers and deep breathing.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Perform passive range-of-motion exercises.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr><tr><td>Avoid external interferences with respiration.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide good room ventilation.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr><tr><td>■ Avoid restrictive clothing, linens, or equipment.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr><tr><td>■ Position in manner conducive to best respiration.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>■ Prevent anxiety-producing situations, such as delays in answering bell call.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NEEDS: Nutrition and Hydration</p>
<p>Nursing Problem: Insufficient dietary intake related to depression and loneliness</p>
<p>Goals: The patient consumes at least <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>500</mn><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1,500~\mathrm{mL}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord">500</span><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mL</span></span></span></span></span></span> of fluids and 1,800 calories of nutrients daily; increases weight to 125 lb.</p>
<p>Nursing Actions</p>
<p>Type of Intervention</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="stimulate-appetite">Stimulate appetite.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stimulate-appetite" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Plan diet according to the person's preferences, consistent with therapeutic. Partially assisting requirements</li>
<li>Provide a quiet, pleasant environment that allows for socialization with others. Doing for</li>
<li>Stimulate appetite through appearance and seasoning of foods. Minimizing self-care limitation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="plan-meals">Plan meals.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plan-meals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Read menu selection to the patient. Partially assisting</li>
<li>Guide the choice of high-protein, carbohydrate, and vitamin- and mineral-rich foods. Minimizing self-care limitation</li>
<li>Access food preferences and include them in menu selections. Acting for</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="assist-with-feeding">Assist with feeding.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assist-with-feeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Conserve energy and promote adequate intake by preparing food tray, encouraging rest periods, and feeding when necessary. Strengthening self-care capacity, doing for, and partially assisting</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevent-complications">Prevent complications.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevent-complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Do not leave solutions, medications, or harmful agents in locations where they may be mistakenly ingested (especially when assessment indicates visual limitations). Acting for</li>
<li>Check the temperature of foods and drinks to prevent burns (especially when assessment indicates decreased cutaneous sensation). Acting for</li>
<li>Assist in the selection of foods conducive to bone healing and correction of malnutrition. Partially assisting</li>
<li>Observe the fluid intake and output for early detection of imbalances. Minimizing self-care limitation</li>
<li>Assess general health status frequently to detect new problems or improvements resulting from changes in nutritional status (e.g., weight changes, skin turgor, mental status, and strength). Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="need-elimination">NEED: Elimination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#need-elimination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nursing Problems: (1) Potential for constipation related to immobility and (2) potential for infection related to malnutrition and interferences with normal bathing</p>
<p>Goals: The patient is free from infection, establishes a regular bowel elimination schedule, is free from constipation, and is clean and odor free.</p>
<p>Nursing Actions</p>
<p>Type of Intervention</p>
<p>Promote regular elimination of bladder and bowels.</p>
<ul>
<li>Guide the selection of a diet high in roughage and fluids. Partially assisting</li>
<li>Observe and record elimination pattern. Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</li>
<li>Assist with exercises to promote peristalsis and urination. Partially assisting</li>
<li>Arrange schedule to provide regular time periods for elimination. Acting for</li>
<li>Assist with hygienic care of body surfaces. Partially assisting</li>
<li>Provide privacy when bedpan is used. Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</li>
</ul>
<p>Develop good hygienic practices.</p>
<ul>
<li>Teach the importance and method of cleansing perineal region after elimination. Strengthening self-care capacity</li>
</ul>
<p>Prevent social isolation.</p>
<ul>
<li>Prevent, detect, and correct body odors. Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="need-movement">NEED: Movement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#need-movement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nursing Problems: (1) Reduced activity level related to malnutrition and fracture and (2) difficulty moving and ambulating related to fracture</p>
<p>Goals: The patient maintains/achieves sufficient range of joint motion to engage in ADL and is free from complications secondary to immobility.</p>
<p>Nursing Actions
Type of Intervention</p>
<p>Adjust hospital routines to individual's pace.</p>
<ul>
<li>Space procedures and other activities. Acting for</li>
<li>Allow longer periods for self-care activities. Strengthening self-care capacity</li>
</ul>
<p>Provide for energy conservation.</p>
<ul>
<li>Promote security and relaxation through the avoidance of frequent changes of personnel. Acting for</li>
<li>Prevent complications associated with immobility (e.g., decubiti, constipation, renal calculi, contractures, hypostatic pneumonia, thrombi, edema, and lethargy). Minimizing self-care limitation</li>
<li>Encourage frequent change of position. Minimizing self-care limitation</li>
<li>Motivate and reward activity. Strengthening self-care capacity</li>
<li>Teach simple exercises to prevent complications and improve motor dexterity. Strengthening self-care capacity</li>
<li>Plan activities to increase independence progressively. Acting for and strengthening self-care capacity</li>
</ul>
<p>NEED: Rest</p>
<p>Nursing Problem: Poor quality and quantity of sleep related to hospital environment and movement limitations associated with fracture</p>
<p>Goals: The patient obtains sufficient sleep to be free from fatigue and learns measures to facilitate sleep and rest.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Control environmental stimuli.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Schedule rest periods between procedures.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>■ Instruct in progressive relaxation.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NEED: Comfort</p>
<p>Nursing Problem: Pain related to fracture</p>
<p>Goals: The patient is free from pain and is able to participate in ADLs without pain-related restrictions.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>■ Monitor for signs of pain.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Assist with positioning and exercises to reduce discomfort.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Plan analgesic administration in collaboration with health care team.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NEED: Immunity</p>
<p>Nursing Problem: Potential for Infection</p>
<p>Goals: The patient is free from infection.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>■ Encourage good food and fluid intake.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Teach the client to include foods in diet that positively affect immune system, such as milk, yogurt, nonfat cottage cheese, eggs, fresh fruits and vegetables, and garlic.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Instruct in and assist with immune-enhancing exercises such as yoga and tai chi.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Review immunization history and arrange for immunizations as needed.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Instruct in stress management techniques.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NEED: Risk Reduction</p>
<p>Nursing Problems: (1) Potential for injury related to sensory deficits; (2) risk of skin breakdown related to immobility, malnutrition, and decreased sensations; and (3) reduced ability to care for home related to altered health state, convalescence</p>
<p>Goals: The patient is free from injury; possesses intact skin; effectively and correctly uses assistive devices, eyeglasses, and hearing aids (as prescribed) to compensate for sensory deficits; and has safe, acceptable living arrangements after discharge.</p>
<p>Nursing Actions</p>
<p>Type of Intervention</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Compensate for poor vision.</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Read to the person.</td><td>Doing for and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Write information and label with large letters and color coding when possible.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Remove obstacles that could cause accidents such as foreign objects in bed, clutter on floor, and solutions that could be mistaken for water.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation and acting for</td></tr><tr><td>Communicate this problem to other personnel.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>Initiate an ophthalmology referral.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>Compensate for decreased ability to smell.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Prevent and correct odors resulting from poor hygienic practices.</td><td>Partially assisting and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Detect unusual odors early (may be symptomatic of infection).</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>Compensate for hearing loss.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Speak clearly and loudly while facing the person.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Use feedback techniques to make sure the person has heard and understood.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Initiate referral to ear, nose, and throat clinic.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>Maintain good skin condition.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Inspect for rashes, reddened areas, and sores.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr><tr><td>Assist with hygienic practices.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Give back rubs, change the person's position frequently, and keep the person's skin soft and dry.</td><td>Doing for, partially assisting, and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Prevent falls.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Support the person who is ambulating or being transported.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Teach and encourage exercises to assist in maintaining muscle tone.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>Keep bed rails up and support the person in wheelchair.</td><td>Doing for</td></tr><tr><td>Provide rest periods between activities.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Place frequently used objects within easy reach.</td><td>Partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Maintain proper body alignment.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Use sandbags, trochanter rolls, and pillows.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation and partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Support the person's affected limb when it is lifted or moved.</td><td>Partially assisting and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>Seek safe living arrangements in preparation for the person's discharge.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Evaluate patient's preferences, capacities, and limitation to suggest appropriate arrangements.</td><td>Acting for and partially assisting</td></tr><tr><td>Initiate referral to social worker.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="need-connection">NEED: Connection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#need-connection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nursing Problems: (1) Emotional and spiritual challenges related to hospitalization, health state, and lifestyle changes and (2) reduced social interactions related to hospitalization and health state</p>
<p>Goals: The patient expresses satisfaction with the amount of social interaction, identifies means for fulfilling spiritual needs, and is free from signs of emotional distress.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Control environmental stimuli.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Schedule the same personnel for caregiving.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>■ Maintain a regular daily schedule.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Arrange for a roommate with similar interests and background.</td><td>Acting for and strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>Promote meaningful social interactions.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Instruct others to speak clearly and sufficiently loud while facing the person.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Plan meaningful activities.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Promote and maintain an oriented state.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Display interest in the person's social interactions and encourage their continuation.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Initiate contacts with community agencies to develop relationships that can continue after discharge.</td><td>Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Assist with grooming and dressing.</td><td>Partially assisting and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Learn about the patient's spirituality and religious beliefs and incorporate this into care.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Arrange for pastoral/spiritual counseling.</td><td>Acting for and strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Encourage verbalization of feelings concerning meaning of health state and life changes.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide opportunities for prayer.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="need-gratification">NEED: Gratification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#need-gratification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nursing Problems: (1) Emotional distress related to hospitalization and health state, (2) reduced social interactions related to hospitalization, and (3) change in self-concept related to health problems and life situation</p>
<p>Goals: The patient demonstrates preinjury level of physical activity, performs self-care activities to maximum level of independence, expresses satisfaction with the amount of solitude, and is free from signs of emotional distress.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nursing Actions</th><th>Type of Intervention</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>■ Control environmental stimuli.</td><td>Acting for</td></tr><tr><td>■ Respect privacy.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td><strong>Provide opportunities for solitude.</strong></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide several preplanned time periods during the day in which the person can be alone.</td><td>Acting for and strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide privacy by pulling curtains around bed and making use of facilities such as chapel.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td><strong>Improve physical limitations where possible.</strong></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Assist with reeducation for ambulation.</td><td>Partially assisting and strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Exercise body parts to maintain function.</td><td>Partially assisting and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Encourage patient to consume an adequate diet.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Initiate referral for audiometric examination to explore utility of hearing aid.</td><td>Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Initiate ophthalmology referral to explore utility of corrective lenses.</td><td>Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td><strong>Maintain familiar components of lifestyle.</strong></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Adjust hospital routine to the person's home routine as much as possible.</td><td>Acting for and minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Encourage the person to wear own clothing.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide the person with personal items from home, pillow, blanket, photographs, and teacup.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation</td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide leisure activities to which the person is accustomed.</td><td>Minimizing self-care limitation and strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td><strong>Promote active participation.</strong></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Provide the person with opportunities to make own decisions whenever possible.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Involve the person in care.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr><tr><td>■ Stimulate and encourage communication.</td><td>Strengthening self-care capacity</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>COMMUNICATION TIP</p>
<p>A skillful assessment and comprehensive care plan mean little if the information remains in the record without being communicated to caregivers. A mechanism should be developed to share the care plan in a format that can be easily used by caregivers and on a level appropriate for them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-healer-role-of-the-nurse">THE HEALER ROLE OF THE NURSE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-healer-role-of-the-nurse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nurses are not merely task-doers but important instruments of their patients' healing process. If completion of tasks was all that constituted nursing care, robots could easily replace nurses. After all, technology exists that could enable a machine to administer a medication, reposition a patient, monitor vital signs, record significant events, and perform other common tasks. Yet the nursing profession emerged as a healing art characterized by its practitioners offering comfort, compassion, support, and caring—factors that were equally (and perhaps sometimes more) important to patients' healing than the procedural tasks of caregiving. Nurses serve a healer role when their interactions assist patients in returning to wholeness (i.e., optimal function and harmony among body, mind, and spirit). Nurses who support holism and healing do not sit on the sidelines as observers; they actively engage in patients' healing processes. This level of engagement is similar to that of the dance instructor who takes the student by the hand and demonstrates the correct steps instead of merely offering directions from the sidelines.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-3bf2682e.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="key-concept">KEY CONCEPT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nurses actively engage in the patient's dance of healing—teaching, guiding, modeling, coaching, encouraging, and helping the patient through the various steps.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="healing-characteristics">Healing Characteristics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#healing-characteristics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Characteristics that enable nurses to engage as healers for older adults include <strong>presence</strong>, availability, willingness to form connections, and being models of holism.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="presence">Presence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The ability to be present in the moment also characterizes nurse healers. Despite the many real activities that nurses typically must complete, the</p>
<p>"busyness" of the average clinical setting, and the unending "to do" list lingering over them, nurse healers are able to protect their interactions with patients from distractions. When with patients, they are with them, giving their full, undivided attention. They actively listen; hear what patients are saying—and not saying—and use their senses to detect subtle clues about needs. Even if the time spent with individual patients is brief, the time fully belongs to their patients.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-9941a9a8.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="point-to-ponder">POINT TO PONDER<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#point-to-ponder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Reflect on an interaction in which the person with whom you were speaking seemed distracted and hurried. How did that influence your communication?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="availability">Availability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#availability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nurse healers display availability of body, mind, and spirit. They provide the time and space for patients to express, explore, and experience. "That's not my job" are words seldom heard from nurse healers. For example, a nurse may be monitoring a patient who is recovering from cataract surgery in an outpatient surgical unit when the patient confides to the nurse that he is distressed at learning that his grandchild was arrested for possession of illegal drugs. A response from the nurse along the lines of "You shouldn't worry about that now" gives the message that the nurse is not available to discuss the patient's concern and most likely will close the door to further discussion. By contrast, responding, "This must be very difficult for you" could be more helpful in conveying openness and interest. Although the nurse in the latter example may not be able to provide all the possible assistance that the patient may require, he or she can allow the patient the safe space to unload this burden on his mind and offer suggestions for follow-up help.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="willingness-to-form-connections">Willingness to Form Connections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#willingness-to-form-connections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nurse healers make connections with their patients. They engage with patients in meaningful ways that require openness, respect, acceptance, and</p>
<p>a nonjudgmental attitude. They commit to learning about what makes each patient a unique individual—the life journey that has been traveled, the story that has formed. At times, this may require that nurses offer insights from their own journeys and share some of the chapters from their lives. Exploring the unique threads that have been woven into the tapestry of a patient's life facilitates connection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="models-of-holism">Models of Holism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#models-of-holism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Effective nurse healers are models of holism, which begins with good self-care practices. They not only eat a proper diet, exercise, obtain adequate rest, and follow other positive health practices but also are attentive to their emotional and spiritual well-being. Integrity demands that nurses know what they want others to know and behave as they want others to behave. Self-care also is essential to performing any other role as a nurse healer.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bringing-research-to-life">BRINGING RESEARCH TO LIFE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bringing-research-to-life" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-establishment-of-a-shared-care-plan-as-it-is-experienced-by-elderly-people-and-their-next-of-kin-a-qualitative-study">The Establishment of a Shared Care Plan as It Is Experienced by Elderly People and Their Next of Kin: A Qualitative Study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-establishment-of-a-shared-care-plan-as-it-is-experienced-by-elderly-people-and-their-next-of-kin-a-qualitative-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Source: Kristensson, J., Andersson, M., &amp; Condelius, A. (2018). Archives of Gerontology and Geriatrics, 79 (6), 131–136.</p>
<p>Nursing outcomes can be improved when older adults with complex needs understand and participate in developing their care plans. Recognizing this fact, the authors of this study explored the process of establishing a shared care plan with older adults and their next of kin.</p>
<p>The study found that the process of establishing a shared care plan fell into three categories: preparation, content, and results. The researchers discovered that efforts to prepare the older adults and their families for the joint care plan meeting were minimal, and often the purpose was unclear to them. The content of the joint care plan meeting tended to be unstructured;</p>
<p>the focus was often on general conversation and practical matters rather than discussion of the plan of care. The researchers concluded that to support person-centered care and produce the desired results, older adults must be an active, equal partner in the process.</p>
<p>Nurses need to consider that even if a care plan looks ideal, it may not have full value if it is not understood or supported by patients. It can be beneficial to arrange uninterrupted time with patients and others whom they would like to participate in their care plan; during that meeting, the nurse can discuss care needs in a manner they can understand, suggest actions and interventions, provide information and instruction as needed, discuss patients' acceptance of those actions and interventions, and adjust as needed. Patients and their significant others are more likely to accept and adhere to a care plan that they understand and contributed to developing than one that is not developed and shared with them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="practice-realities">PRACTICE REALITIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#practice-realities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As a new staff member of a nursing home, you notice that other staff make decisions and perform activities for many residents who seem capable of doing these things for themselves. When caring for some of these residents, you give them the opportunity to make choices about their preferences, which they have been pleased and able to make. In addition, when encouraging them to feed themselves, residents have performed the task, although more time was required to complete care.</p>
<p>What could be the possible reasons for staff creating unnecessary dependence in the residents? How could you encourage a change in their approaches?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="critical-thinking-exercises">CRITICAL THINKING EXERCISES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#critical-thinking-exercises" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Identify life experiences that have been unique to today's older population and that have prepared them to cope with some of the</li>
</ol>
<p>challenges of old age.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>List age-related changes that could affect each of the health promotion-related needs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>What are some reasons for older adults not wanting to function independently in self-care activities?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Describe some situations in which older adults are at risk for losing independence as a result of nurses doing for them rather than promoting independence.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-summary">Chapter Summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Holistic gerontological care integrates the biologic, psychological, social, and spiritual dimensions of an individual in which the synergy creates a sum that is greater than its parts. It is concerned with not only the treatment of illnesses but also the facilitation of growth toward wholeness, maximization of quality of life, and the provision of peace, comfort, and dignity during the dying process.</p>
<p>Holistic gerontological nursing assessment considers physiological balance, the connection of the individual with self, others, the culture, and the environment, and the degree to which the person is achieving gratification. When health challenges are present, the individual may present new needs, such as for education, counseling, coaching, monitoring, coordination, therapies, and advocacy. The requisites that must be present for the individual to meet these needs are physical, mental, and socioeconomic abilities; knowledge, experience, and skills; and the desire and decision to take action.</p>
<p>Nurses need to recognize the considerable inner resources that older adults possess and mobilize these resources to actively engage these individuals in their own care. Doing so will empower older adults and facilitate commitment to the plan of care.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-8">CHAPTER 8<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-8" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="legal-aspects-of-gerontological-nursing">Legal Aspects of Gerontological Nursing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#legal-aspects-of-gerontological-nursing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
